Free-Market Money, Courtesy of the Web
Free-Market Money, Courtesy of the Web
By Bob Gelfond
701 words
7 June 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The digital currency called Bitcoin may or may not survive long-term, but it has already succeeded on one
front: making people think seriously about alternative forms of money. More such alternatives to traditional
currency will likely emerge.
The attraction of the Bitcoin is not just the anonymity it provides to users (an anonymity that the U.S.
government has alleged some users have employed for money laundering). Bitcoin is also secure against
traditional forms of counterfeiting. More important, the Bitcoin is designed to be scarce and thus immune to
inflation. There is a limit to the number of Bitcoins -- 21 million -- that is determined by a transparent rule and
not by the whim of a central banker.
Gold used to serve this purpose. It was a commodity that was relatively scarce and not easily mined, thus
reducing the risk of inflation. That valuable function has been superseded by today's paper moneys. In this
sense, Bitcoin is an electronic version of gold.
In the future, it is likely that other digital alternatives to currency will emerge, each one competing in the
market to satisfy the needs of users. The possibilities are limited only by the imagination. Here is one:
Cloud-computing companies could issue certificates convertible into an hour of premium computing. Prices
for other services could be quoted in terms of these certificates. The analogy is to traditional gold-backed
currency, which was redeemable into physical gold at the option of the owner. While the dollar price of an
hour of premium computing would vary with market conditions, the certificate would be guaranteed to always
convert into one hour of premium computing. The maxim "time is money" would take on a new meaning.
Computing has become a fundamental element of virtually every technology, a common currency, so to
speak, of doing business in today's economy. Cloud-computing companies provide on-demand storage and
computing at a price that varies with market conditions and the computing speed required. They compete on
price and the quality and reliability of their cloud, which can be accessed from anywhere in the world at any
time. Competition forces the companies to continually upgrade to the latest generation of computers and
data-storage facilities.
Cloud-computing certificates could be redeemed at any time. The owner of a one-hour certificate, for
example, could convert the certificate today or wait 18 months when presumably an hour of computing time
would be able to do twice as much because of the increased computing speed that comes from Moore's Law.
The certificates would never expire, so the owner need never convert them. Unlike gift certificates, they would
not be fixed in price to a certain dollar value but to a fixed amount of computing time. If the public gained
confidence that the cloud-computing companies were not flooding the market with certificates, the certificates
might be viewed as stable and liquid enough to circulate as money. If the certificates were freely
transferrable, they could come to be used to buy any product or service, or be saved as a stable store of
value.
As confidence and familiarity in the certificates grew, the certificates could even be used as a basis for credit
or any other thing that dollars are used for. Certificates would have an advantage over Federal
Reserve-produced dollars as their supply and price would be completely dictated by the market.
During slow economic times, cloud-computing companies would have less incentive to expand their clouds
and would reduce the number of certificates they issued. The reverse would happen in better economic
times. The certificates would have an advantage over the Bitcoin: They could be converted into something of
value, instead of just being based on relative scarcity.
It is impossible to predict what kinds of money a truly free market will create in an increasingly digitized world.
But we can be confident in predicting that just as markets improve the quality of all products, they will do the
same for money.

Technologies, which commercializes quantum information technologies.

Paulson: All That Glitters Isn't Gold
Paulson: All That Glitters Isn't Gold
By Gregory Zuckerman and Juliet Chung
776 words
7 June 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
John Paulson has a message for investors: Stop paying so much attention to my gold bets.
Mr. Paulson, the billionaire hedge-fund manager who has been one of the most bullish investors in the
precious metal and suffered deep losses as a result, is eager to focus attention on his better-performing
investments, which also dwarf his firm's gold fund in size.
Now, his $18 billion firm, Paulson & Co . will stop including the performance of the gold fund when it shares
monthly updates with investors in its healthier funds, according to a letter sent to his investors Thursday.
From now on, investors in the gold fund, which manages about $360 million, will receive separate word of the
fund's returns, the letter says.
Mr. Paulson, the firm's founder, has grown frustrated that his firm's gold troubles have obscured much-better
returns from his other funds. Paulson Gold was down 47% for the year through April, including a 26.5%
decline in April. The firm hasn't told investors about May's returns for the gold fund yet.
"At the request of clients and consultants, we will be reporting the performance of our Gold Funds separately
to investors in those funds and interested parties," the letter says, noting that those funds represent only 2%
of assets under management. The funds "have received a disproportionate amount of attention over recent
months and have detracted attention from the performance and positive developments of our other funds."
The firm will begin conducting separate conference calls for the gold fund. It also plans to stop broadly
reporting the performance of the gold share class of its various funds in its regular investor updates, though it
will share those figures with clients who are invested in the gold-share class or with people who specifically
ask for it. The gold share classes were introduced by Mr. Paulson to give all of his investors access to the
metal.
One of Mr. Paulson's other funds has been on a tear, while others have held up better than gold, spurring the
move. The Paulson Recovery fund, for example, which manages about $2 billion and invests in companies
that benefit from a broad economic upturn, jumped 4.9% in May and is up 27% on the year, according to the
letter to investors. The fund has made money on "insurance, banking, and defaulted securities," it said.
Paulson & Co .'s two credit funds, his biggest, rose 3.6% in May and are up 16.2% for the year through May.
They have profited from bets on defaulted and convertible securities, the investor letter says.
Meanwhile, his merger funds are up between 8.2% and 17.4% through May, profiting from bets on various
deals.
Still, Paulson Advantage and Paulson Advantage Plus funds, which bet on anticipated corporate events and
manage about $3.6 billion, also have been hit by the decline in gold because of positions in gold stocks.
Those funds are up 2.4% and 3.3%, respectively, in May. The Paulson Advantage fund is up 4.4% for the
year, while Paulson Advantage Plus is up 6.1% in 2013.
"Lots of people are beating up on him because he's been wrong on gold for some time, but he has many
different strategies, and those have been performing pretty well," said Vidak Radonjic of Beryl Consulting
Group LLC, who advises on hedge-fund investing. Mr. Radonjic, who recommends his clients invest with
smaller managers because he believes they are more nimble, nonetheless said he found the gains of Mr.
Paulson's larger funds impressive.
Mr. Paulson made his name scoring $20 billion of profits over 2007 and 2008 by betting against subprime
mortgages and financial companies ahead of the financial collapse.
Gold prices have fallen 15.5% in 2013, despite a rise of 1.2% on Thursday. As stocks have climbed and
inflation has remained tame, investors have dumped gold-tied investments.

--- Page 119 ---
Page 119 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The shift in reporting doesn't mean Mr. Paulson has reduced his commitment to gold. He isn't selling gold
investments, according to people close to the matter, citing what he considers to be an attractive valuation for
many gold-mining companies, which have slumped for several years.
Heavy losses from gold and other investments in Mr. Paulson's Advantage funds made them much smaller,
putting more of a focus on the Recovery fund and other better performers.
He also remains bullish on real estate, believing the current turnaround will continue for as many as four or
five years, people familiar with the matter said.

Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Monthly Analysis --- The Experts: Insights From Our Online Panel
Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Monthly Analysis --- The Experts: Insights From Our Online Panel
1,808 words
3 June 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
R4
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Should the average investor have cash in an investment portfolio? What about gold? And for that matter, if
investors want to manage their own money, what is the most valuable advice a professional could give them?
For answers to such questions, The Wall Street Journal turned to The Experts, an exclusive group of industry
and thought leaders who engage in in-depth online discussions of topics from the print Report. Edited
excerpts follow:
---
Should You Keep Cash in
Your Portfolio?
In a Word, Absolutely.
My rule of thumb for investors is that if you think you may need the funds within the next three years, then
those dollars should probably be left in cash or cash equivalents. You can't afford to risk losing money in the
stock or bond market with dollars you might actually need within a few years.
-- Michelle Perry Higgins, financial planner and principal at California Financial Advisors
Respond to Opportunities
Cash provides flexibility during times when markets come under pressure. Central banks around the world
have driven the returns on cash to zero, and have caused distortions in the prices of more volatile assets. No
one knows whether those policies will lead to nirvana or catastrophe. Sacrificing a little upside by holding
extra cash right now is a good trade for having a cushion if this grand experiment goes awry.
-- Tom Brakke, consultant, writer and investment adviser
Cash Doesn't Earn a Thing
Cash, after taxes and inflation, doesn't earn anything. Today, inflation is higher than the return on cash.
-- Rick Ferri, author, blogger and founder of Portfolio Solutions LLC
When Opportunity Knocks
I always like to have some cash lying around to take advantage of opportunities that may arise suddenly.
Market corrections will always occur; you just never know when.
-- George Papadopoulos, fee-only wealth manager in Novi, Mich.
Not an Investment
Put cash in your savings account and investments in your portfolio. The only cash you may need in your
investment portfolio is money to fund distributions, to take advantage of opportunities, or maybe you've simply
met your objective or it's matured and you're awaiting reinvestment. Otherwise, cash is not an investment.
-- Sheryl Garrett , founder of Garrett Planning Network Inc.
More Important With Age
An allocation to cash is increasingly important as investors get closer to retirement. Particularly now,
investors may opt for a little more cash at the expense of fixed income, given the nosebleed valuations in

--- Page 123 ---
Page 123 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
government bonds. Even for ultra-aggressive investors, a modest cash allocation gives you some ammunition
when short-term market volatility reveals attractive bargains.
-- Greg McBride , senior financial analyst and vice president for Bankrate.com
Seven-Year Rule
Money you know you'll need to spend in the next seven years should not be invested in stocks or bonds.
Rather it should be parked for safe keeping in cash equivalents. This frees you up to take full advantage of
the risk/reward characteristics inherent in equity investing. However, if you are referring to your IRA or your
401(k) as your investment portfolio and you are not within seven years of planning to take distributions, then I
prefer to see portfolios fully invested.
-- Manisha Thakor, founder and chief executive of MoneyZen Wealth Management LLC
Leave It at 5% or Less
Unless you are a very conservative investor, I would keep the allocation to cash and cash equivalents to a
modest amount -- say 5% or less. The primary reason for a cash allocation would be to try to move into and
out of the market (i.e., market timing). Studies show consistently that market timing is rarely, if ever,
successful.
-- Larry D. Zimpleman , chairman, president and chief executive of Principal Financial Group , chairman,
president and chief executive of
---
Should the Average Investor
Buy Gold?
It's a Bad Investment. And Yet . . .
Gold is only worth what the next buyer is willing to pay. So, why do I hold a smidgen of gold in my portfolio?
The shiny metal has returns that aren't correlated with either stocks or bonds. So, gold does offer a
diversification benefit. But, I still hate it as an investment.
-- Charles Rotblut , a vice president with the American Association of Individual Investors , a vice president
with the
Put 5% in Precious Metals
A small allocation of 5% is an appropriate holding for most investors. But buying actual gold and precious
metals increases your costs for storage and insurance. Consider instead a mutual fund or exchange-traded
fund that holds actual precious metals, rather than those investing in futures contracts or mining companies
that introduce additional risks you aren't prepared for.
-- Greg McBride
For Unstable Economies
Gold doesn't belong in the portfolio of the average U.S. investor. Gold doesn't produce anything. It isn't a
company providing services or goods. It isn't farmland providing food. The same advice wouldn't necessarily
apply to someone in a politically or economically unstable country. While such a person would face the same
sentiment risk when buying gold, this risk could be less than the risk of currency collapse.
-- Terrance Odean, Rudd Family Foundation professor and chairman of the finance group at Haas School of
Business, University of California, Berkeley
What's the Fair Value?
Gold is a difficult asset to determine a fair value for. There is some reason to believe that gold can act as a
store of value during stressed times -- so if you are conservative in your investment outlook and you believe
that all markets are overvalued, then there may be a place for a modest exposure to gold as an alternative to
cash. But -- for an average investor -- I wouldn't see them wanting exposure to gold.
-- Larry D. Zimpleman
Doomsayers and Speculators

--- Page 124 ---
Page 124 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Doomsayers have been predicting the end of the world ever since 1933 when the dollar went off the gold
standard. They are in the fear-and-doom prediction business. It has been known to sell better than sex. The
only problem is the doomsayers have always been wrong. Wealth managers always have a few clients who
sleep better knowing they have up to 5% invested in gold or other precious metals. Doing so in an ETF like
the iShares Gold Trust is preferable.
-- George Papadopoulos
Not Even Dentists Still Use Gold
When you invest in gold, what are you investing in? It isn't a productive enterprise. Yes, it is pretty when
fashioned into rings or necklaces, but not even dentists still use it for filling teeth.
In times of crisis, it is a go-to for nervous investors, but so are U.S. Treasurys. People argue that it is a store
of value, but not intrinsically so. It only stores value if you and I and most everyone else agree it is the one
and only store.
-- Eleanor Blayney , consumer advocate of the Certified Financial Planner Board of Standards
There Are Better Hedges
If your purpose for buying gold is to hedge inflation, most advisers recommend holding 5% to 10% of your
portfolio in gold. The fractional interest you own wouldn't make a real dent in the impact of inflation. Some
stock exposure, home equity and living debt-free are more effective hedges against inflation for the average
American. To add an inflation hedge to your portfolio, consider real assets (commodities) and Treasury
inflation-protected securities (TIPS).
-- Sheryl Garrett
---
What Is the Best Way to
Invest on Your Own?
Study Financial History
It is unlikely you'll do very well fighting it out in the financial markets against those with more experience and
information than you have. If you still want to try your luck, start reading, but skip the how-to-beat-the-market
books and spend your time learning financial history. Perspective and a long time horizon are what you need
most, although you'll probably end up making the same mistakes that the rest of us make anyway.
-- Tom Brakke
Invest for the Long Haul
Invest for the long haul and build the core of your portfolio with mutual funds and exchange-traded funds
rather than individual securities. Keep investment expenses low by favoring index funds and no-load mutual
funds, and keep trading to a minimum.
-- Greg McBride
Learn to Golf Instead
My advice to a prospective active do-it-yourself investor is to learn to golf. You'll get a little exercise, some
fresh air and time with your friends. Sure, green fees can be steep, but not as steep as the hit your portfolio
will take if you become an active do-it-yourself investor.
---
Terrance Odean
Strategy, Then Tactics
It is crucial to educate yourself. Differentiate between literature on investment strategy and literature on
investment tactics. Here, I'm drawing a lesson from chess. Chess strategy broadly focuses on the long term:
What are the sound fundamentals that will guide my specific plans and moves to the ultimate goal of
checkmate? Chess tactics, on the other hand, narrowly focus on the short term: What specific moves will
enable me to control a particular part of the board, capture a specific piece, etc.?

--- Page 125 ---
Page 125 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
-- Rafael Pardo, Robert T. Thompson professor of law at Emory University , specializing in bankruptcy and
commercial law
Four Steps to Better Returns
Be realistic about your interest level in finance and the amount of time you can devote to managing a
portfolio.
Consider both your financial and your emotional tolerance for risk.
Use the power of the written word to your advantage: Write down the reasons that cause you to favorably
view an investment and the reasons you would sell it before you make the actual purchase.
Pick good securities and diversify. Look for securities of financially strong companies trading at discounted
valuations.
Do these four simple things and your future investment returns will be better.
-- Charles Rotblut
Answer Three Questions
I'd give the exact same advice I'd give to any investor, which is to make sure you can answer these three
questions at all times: (1) What is my asset allocation? (2) What has my performance been? (3) What fees
am I paying?
-- Manisha Thakor
Know When to Fold
To be a successful manager of your own investments, you need to be disciplined, humble and dispassionate.
You need to systematically rebalance the portfolio and be willing to admit your mistakes or -- at the very least
-- that great stocks, like great athletes, cannot stay on top forever. You need to have a sell strategy in place
whenever you buy a new holding. You especially need to be careful never to fall in love with your choices,
and at the same time, never allow yourself to hate taxes to the point that the tax tail has wagged the dog to
death.
-- Eleanor Blayney

Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Monthly Analysis --- What Are You Afraid Of? --- You can take steps to cushion your portfolio against the risks...
Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Monthly Analysis --- What Are You Afraid Of? --- You can take steps to
cushion your portfolio against the risks that worry you the most
By Michael A. Pollock
1,532 words
3 June 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
It has taken four years for stocks to recover from their 2009 lows. So, can you rest easy?
Hardly. With markets trading at warp speed, a new shock could vaporize a big chunk of your hard-won equity
gains in minutes.
Consider how the market might react to a sudden war or major natural disaster. Could your portfolio handle
that? How about another flare-up of the euro-zone fiscal crisis? Or the long-feared bump in interest rates?
Even something as relatively mild as a stock-market correction could do real damage, as could the current
no-yield, fixed-income environment if it stretches on for years.
Other than holding a lot of cash -- which pays next to nothing and will lose value once you factor in inflation
-- there isn't any easy way of hedging against every conceivable danger. But some mutual-fund and
exchange-traded-fund strategies could cushion the impact while keeping you in the game if markets continue
to rally.
Certain equity funds, for instance, might make your stockholdings more resilient. Some types of bond funds
could be a safer bet in an environment of rising -- or persistent near-0% -- interest rates. And you could add
other assets that wouldn't move in sync with stocks or bonds.
Here are some strategies that might help you hedge against whatever scenario you most fear.
If Stocks Suddenly Start Sliding
Because equity indexes already have gained about 15% this year, a pullback wouldn't be surprising, says
Lewis Altfest, principal adviser at Altfest Personal Wealth Management in New York.
You may suffer smaller losses, he says, if you cut back on small- and midcap stocks and shift more to
blue-chips that consistently pay dividends and have global reach. These types of stocks tend to be steadier
bets during uncertain times.
Consider investing in an ETF such as SPDR S&P Dividend, which owns shares of companies that have a
record of steadily increasing their dividends, or Guggenheim Russell Top 50 Mega Cap, which focuses on 50
of the largest-cap U.S. stocks. You also might try investing in an ETF that favors less-volatile stocks.
If a Natural Disaster or War
Sparks Market Panic
Keeping at least some of your portfolio in fixed-income securities offers one of the best long-term
counterbalances to equity volatility, says Ben Johnson, global director of passive-funds research at
Morningstar Inc . High-quality bonds such as Treasurys typically rally in turbulent times -- although at their
current levels, they likely have less room for appreciation than in the past.
Investors also should consider an allocation to an absolute-return strategy, which aims to make money
regardless of the direction of stock and bond markets, says Jeffrey Knight , head of global asset allocation at
Columbia Management.
"They can add another dimension of diversification," he says. The downside is that these funds, which include
long-short funds that bet that some securities will rise and others fall, often charge relatively high expenses of
2% to 3% a year of assets.

--- Page 128 ---
Page 128 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Gateway fund writes covered calls, which are options that give their owner the right to buy a stock at a
certain price by a specified date. The fund gets additional income from selling the options, and can pay that
income out to fund holders. Gateway uses some of its income from selling covered calls to buy downside
equity protection. It returned an annualized 6.5% over the past three years, and the net expense ratio is
0.94%.
Another relatively low-cost option is Glenmede Total Market, a long-short fund with a 1.25% expense ratio
and a 4.5% five-year average annual return.
If U.S. Interest Rates Jump When
the Fed Reduces Stimulus
Bond yields move in the opposite direction of their prices and have stayed low because of the Federal
Reserve's massive purchases of bonds in an attempt to keep borrowing costs down and stimulate economic
activity. But yields could rebound once the Fed pulls away from that policy, and that would erode the principal
value of any bonds you own.
Bond funds, with no final maturity, are particularly vulnerable. One that owns intermediate maturities could
lose 4% to 5% of principal value if rates broadly rose one percentage point.
Consider switching to a fund with a tactical focus that can shift holdings to reduce risk, says Jerry Villella, who
oversees client portfolios at J.P. Morgan Private Bank in Dallas. Among flexible funds are Pimco Total
Return, which also comes in an ETF version, and Pimco Unconstrained Bond.
Shorter-maturity bonds will lose less value in the event of a rise in interest rates. Philip Wagner, portfolio
manager at the Bryn Mawr Trust unit of Bryn Mawr Bank Corp ., near Philadelphia, says investors might put
60% of their bond bucket into Vanguard Short-Term Investment-Grade and 40% into Vanguard
Intermediate-Term Investment-Grade. The result would be a blended rate sensitivity about half that of the
typical core bond fund.
If Rates Remain Low for Years
Some bond strategists think sluggish global economies and strong demand for income will keep a lid on bond
yields for a long time.
In that case, you might look to a multisector bond fund that invests in many markets, such as lower-rated
"junk" debt and foreign bonds, in search of higher yields. Among these is Loomis Sayles Bond, which yields
about 3% and ranks in the top 10% of Morningstar's multisector bond grouping for the past 10 years.
Or consider a closed-end fund that invests in dividend-paying stocks and generates additional income by
selling calls. (Unlike their open-end fund cousins, closed-end funds sell a set number of shares at origination
and trade on stock exchanges.)
If you choose a closed-end fund trading at a discount to its net asset value, you might also get some capital
appreciation, says Maury Fertig, chief investment officer at Relative Value Partners LLC, Northbrook, Ill.
Mr. Fertig suggests Nuveen Equity Premium & Growth Common, yielding nearly 8% and trading at an 8%
discount to its net asset value. Another possibility is BlackRock Enhanced Capital & Income, yielding nearly
9% and trading at about a 9% discount to net asset value.
If the Economy Slows So Much That Deflation Threatens
Although averting deflation (that is, falling consumer prices) is central to the Federal Reserve's current policy,
it is uncertain how effective that policy will be in sparking economic growth. In a deflationary climate, the
outperformers would be high-quality bonds -- the same ones you wouldn't want to own if rates were to surge.
TCW Core Fixed-Income keeps about two-thirds of its holdings in top-rated triple-A bonds, including
mortgage securities. The fund ranks in the top 15% of Morningstar's intermediate-bond category for the past
10 years.
Alternatively, you could add an ETF such as Vanguard Intermediate-Term Government Bond or iShares
Barclays 20+ Year Treasury Bond, which both focus on government securities.
If Inflation Surges as
Central Banks Pump Out Monetary Stimulus

--- Page 129 ---
Page 129 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
If inflation flares, you might want to invest in shares of consumer-staple companies like Coca-Cola Co ., says
Mr. Altfest, the New York adviser. Such companies likely could pass along increased costs of raw materials to
their customers. An ETF focused on such stocks is iShares S&P Global Consumer Staples. Its holdings
include Nestle SA , Procter & Gamble Co . and Coca-Cola .
You could also add some Treasury inflation-protected securities, or TIPS -- Treasurys whose principal value
and coupon payments rise along with the Consumer Price Index. Avoid long maturities, which could suffer if
yields surge. Instead, consider funds like Pimco 1-5 Year U.S. TIPS Index or Vanguard Short-Term
Inflation-Protected Securities ETF, which own shorter maturities.
If Europe's Fiscal Crisis
Erupts Again
Although moves by the European Central Bank appear to have damped worries about a euro-zone breakup,
concerns remain. One fear is voters in a major euro-zone nation might flock to a political party that advocates
an exit from the euro, says Bill McQuaker , who oversees multi-asset strategies at Henderson Global
Investors Ltd ., part of Henderson Group PLC in London. Renewed worries about rising anti-euro sentiment
would unsettle equities markets again, Mr. McQuaker says.
As in other situations, high-quality bonds would help soften the impact of stock gyrations. But Mr. McQuaker
says investors might also consider adding a modest allocation to gold, which has historically drawn strong
demand at times of high political or economic uncertainty.
Gold ETFs themselves can be volatile, and gains on precious-metal ETFs held for more than one year are
taxed at a rate as high as 28%.
But with that in mind, it might be worth looking at iShares Gold Trust or SPDR Gold Shares as a way of
owning the yellow metal.
---
Mr. Pollock is a writer in Ridgewood, N.J. Email him at reports@wsj.com.

World News: India Growth Slows to Decade Low, Pressing Government
World News: India Growth Slows to Decade Low, Pressing Government
By Anant Vijay Kala and Mukesh Jagota
634 words
1 June 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A6
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW DELHI -- India's economy grew at its slowest annual rate in a decade, building pressure on the
government to take more ambitious action to reinvigorate the ailing economy.
Gross domestic product rose 5.0% in the fiscal year ended March 31, after a 6.2% increase in 2012 and
annual rates of over 9% for several years before the global financial crisis of 2008.
The weak data will add pressure on the Congress party-led national coalition government. The government
already is facing criticism that it has inadequately sought to tackle persistent problems that have dragged on
growth, including high inflation and burgeoning fiscal and current-account deficits.
Boosting growth will be key for the Congress party to improve its chances of being re-elected for a third
consecutive term at national polls scheduled to be held before next May.
While growth of 5% would be high for a developed Western economy, such a rate is insufficient in India to
create enough new jobs for a young workforce.
Data also released Friday showed output grew 4.8% on year from January to March, in line with market
expectations and a touch quicker than 4.7% in the preceding quarter.
Robert Prior-Wandesforde, an Asia-based economist with Credit Suisse , said there was some cause for
optimism in those numbers but that the data don't warrant the "popping of champagne corks."
India's economic growth powered ahead in the mid-2000s as investors warmed to a country that appeared to
be throwing off its Socialist-inspired economic policies and opening up to greater foreign investment.
But in the past few years the narrative has changed. Foreign businesses have shunned the nation amid
concerns about the slow pace of economic changes to make it easier to invest in the country. A focus instead
on state-led social spending has widened the fiscal deficit, while huge oil imports have ballooned the trade
gap.
That in turn, has hurt the rupee, which is currently trading at an 11-month low of 56.50. At the same time,
inflation of near double digits has forced the central bank to keep interest rates high. Exports, too, have
fared poorly due to the slowdown in the U.S. and Europe. Even the country's IT outsourcing companies, once
the symbol of shining India, have begun to face headwinds.
Also Friday, the government said it narrowed its fiscal deficit to 4.9% of GDP in the most-recent fiscal year,
below the 5.2% estimate made in the federal budget announced in February. But the level is still well above
the government's 3% target and has led ratings agencies to warn that India's sovereign debt could soon be
downgraded to noninvestment-grade status.
Prime Minister Manmohan Singh last fall unveiled a series of reforms meant to kick-start the economy,
including allowing greater investment in sectors such as retail, broadcasting and aviation. The government
also has sought to improve its finances by lowering expensive state subsidies on fuel and raising taxes to
curb gold imports, the main driver of India's record-high trade deficit.
Many of these measures have been neutralized by a growing protectionist sentiment in the country. Many
Indian states, for instance, have opposed allowing foreign supermarkets to set up and have opted not to
apply these regulations in their regions.
Businesses have for months been calling on the Reserve Bank of India -- the central bank -- to cut rates to
jump-start growth. The RBI, worried about inflation and encouraging more imports, has cut its key lending
rate by 0.75 percentage point this year, much less aggressively than industry leaders wanted.

U.S. Stocks Weather a Storm --- Dow Moves Up Despite Another Big Down Day in Japan; Bank, Tech
Shares Win
By Chris Dieterich and Brad Frischkorn

The Wall Street Journal

C1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks sat out a market rout in Tokyo for the third time in just over a week, shrugging off mixed economic
data at home to notch a modest advance.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average advanced 21.73 points, or 0.1%, to 15324.53, rebounding from a
107-point decline Wednesday.
Thursday's rise came after the Nikkei Stock Average skidded 5.2% overnight, led by losses among exporters,
as investors questioned the extent to which the government's economic-recovery programs are likely to boost
growth. Japanese stocks rebounded early Friday, with the Nikkei up more than 2%,
Thursday's selloff pushed the Japanese market rally into a correction phase, with shares down double digits
percentage-wise, and more than a month's gains were wiped out. The drop had no clear cause, hitting in the
afternoon in a move that didn't appear directly linked to any news developments.
Among those suffering from the selloff: global hedge funds. In recent months, a range of hedge funds, from
Hugh Hendry's Eclectica Fund to Daniel Loeb 's Third Point LLC , have piled into Japanese stocks, betting
the Abe administration would succeed in generating growth and that a recovery was just starting.
At a recent industry conference, Michael Novogratz , a principal at Fortress Investment Group LLC , said the
bulk of the profits of so-called macro hedge funds, or those wagering on macroeconomic events, were
coming from Japan.
Now, many hedge funds are feeling pain, along with Japanese retail investors, traders say.
After Thursday's drop, the Nikkei had lost 13% since last Wednesday, after having climbed more than 70%
since the start of November. Japan's market has been on a tear since Prime Minister Shinzo Abe announced
late last year his program to revitalize the economy, which has been beset for two decades with anemic
growth and falling prices that discourage spending.
Thursday's decline was the third big drop over the past week, following a 7.3% tumble Thursday of last week
and a 3.2% slide this past Monday.
But the tumble hasn't shaken many investors convinced Japan remains a viable opportunity.
"Our house view is that this is a healthy pullback," said Adam Grossman, director of asset-allocation research
at RiverFront Investment Group , which is standing pat on its outsize position in Japanese stocks.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index gained 6.05 points, or 0.4%, to 1654.41, and the Nasdaq Composite
Index tacked on 23.78 points, or 0.7%, to 3491.30.
A revision to U.S. first-quarter gross domestic product, a broad measure of the economy, showed a slight dip
in growth, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 2.4%, from an initial estimate of 2.5%. Every data point is
being closely examined after Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke indicated last week that the central
bank could pare back its stimulus efforts should the economy continue to show signs of improvement.
"The GDP revision [Thursday] morning was pretty benign, and the outlook remains mostly unchanged," said
Clayton Albright, director of asset allocation at Wilmington Trust Investment Advisors . Mr. Albright said his
firm has been selling investment-grade bonds and buying global stocks this year.

Currencies Cave to Commodity Dive --- Aussie Dollar, Others Start to Tumble as Investors Bet on Sustained Price Declines for Raw Materials

Currencies Cave to Commodity Dive --- Aussie Dollar, Others Start to Tumble as Investors Bet on
Sustained Price Declines for Raw Materials
By Ira Iosebashvili
748 words
30 May 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Currencies of the world's biggest commodity exporters are plumbing fresh lows, the latest sign that investors
are betting on a sustained decline in prices of oil, iron ore, copper and other raw materials.
In the past month, currencies as far-flung as the Australian dollar, Canadian dollar and Chilean peso have
seen big losses as the U.S. dollar began a broad rally driven by expectations that the Federal Reserve would
pull back on its bond buying later this year.
These currencies had held their value for much of the past two years, even as prices for copper, gold and oil
slipped from their highs.
Now, though, some currency investors are capitulating because the commodity-price decline has been long
enough and steep enough that it is starting to show up in countries' trade balances, government finances and
growth forecasts. Meanwhile, the prospect of an end to the Fed's easy-money policies is removing one of the
remaining incentives to hold these currencies.
"There is quite a bit more downside to this," said Chris Stanton, a portfolio manager at Sunrise Capital, a
$400 million fund based in San Diego. "You will soon start to see these currencies really bleed."
Mr. Stanton said he is buying the U.S. dollar while selling the Australian and New Zealand dollars and the
South African rand.
The Dow Jones-UBS Commodity Index, which tracks prices for 22 goods, is down 5.5% this year and 25%
from its recent peak in May 2011.
Most currencies of commodities-exporting countries have only begun to feel the effects. The Chilean peso, for
instance, was up 1.5% against the dollar this year through the start of May, a stretch when the price of
copper, the country's biggest export, tumbled 15%.
Currencies such as the peso have been propped up by the Fed's stimulus program, which allows investors to
borrow cheaply in dollars and buy assets in countries that offer better returns. In the years after the 2008
financial crisis, commodities-exporting economies also performed better, buoyed by strong demand for raw
materials from China. That led some investors to view currencies such as oil exporter Norway's krone as a
refuge from instability in the euro zone and a sluggish U.S. recovery.
The benefits of holding these currencies have faded this year, investors say. China's economy is showing
signs of slowing, causing commodities prices to slide. On May 22, Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke said he
would consider winding down the central bank's easing program this year if the economy continues to
improve.
"These currencies became very overextended in the past few years, when they were seen as safe havens
because they were backed by assets" in the form of commodities, said Charles Morris, who manages the
$2.5 billion International Select family of funds for HSBC . "But now the value of these assets is going down,
and the currencies are following suit."
Mr. Morris is betting the dollar will continue to rise. He said he has almost no exposure to emerging markets,
which tend to rely more on commodities exports.
To be sure, not all the currencies of top commodities exporters can be viewed the same, analysts say. Some
see developed economies weathering a period of low commodities prices better than emerging markets.
"Countries such as Canada and Australia are too diversified, their capital markets too broad, for their

--- Page 135 ---
Page 135 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
currencies to be driven by commodity prices alone," said Marc Chandler , a strategist at Brown Brothers
Harriman in New York.
However, the effects of lower prices is being felt even in relatively diverse markets. On Wednesday, the
Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development forecast iron and coal exporter Australia's growth
to slow to 2.6% this year, from 3.6% in 2012. Brazil reported a $6.2 billion trade deficit this year through April,
compared with a $3.3 billion surplus over the same four months last year.
Anticipating a weaker economy, Australia's central bank earlier this month cut interest rates to a record low
2.75%. The Australian dollar is down 7.4% in May.
Other central banks are seen cutting rates as the benefits of the commodities boom ebb.
"These rates are not that appealing anymore," said Joon Hyuk Heo, head of global fixed income at Mirae
Asset Global Investments.

As Syrian Islamists Gain, It's Rebel Against Rebel
As Syrian Islamists Gain, It's Rebel Against Rebel
By Nour Malas
2,158 words
30 May 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
RAQQA, Syria -- In parts of this battle-torn country, a second civil war has begun.
In the north and east bordering Turkey and Iraq, a stretch of Syria in rebel hands has split into competing
fiefs. In some places, Islamist extremists with agendas that extend far beyond Syria are pushing aside the
rebel battalions that started the insurgency against Bashar al-Assad's regime in 2011.
The resulting chaos has fragmented the rebel fight and created a playground for al Qaeda just as a U.S.
debate heats up over how deeply to get involved.
In Raqqa, a dusty province in the country's northeastern breadbasket, the capital city in March went from
pro-regime to rebel-controlled in a matter of weeks. Islamists almost simultaneously gained the upper hand,
and on March 22, the black flag of al Qaeda appeared above the main square.
The al-Nusra Front, an al Qaeda affiliate that espouses global Islamic rule and is labeled terrorist by the U.S.,
now runs the largest bakery. Another Islamist group called Ahrar al-Sham runs buses and controls the local
branch of the Syrian Central Bank .
Meanwhile, in towns that dot the road west to Aleppo province, ethnic Kurds suspicious of new Islamist power
brokers have put up many more checkpoints. Clashes increasingly break out between Kurdish factions and
al-Nusra.
The Islamist takeover of Raqqa has already ushered in what many residents and rebels describe as Syria's
new war, between moderate anti-Assad rebels and those bent on imposing religious governance. It is a turf
war some locals believe could shape Syria's future as profoundly as the struggle against the regime.
U.S. intelligence is scrambling to get a better handle on the situation. The Central Intelligence Agency has
held covert conversations with some of these fighters, including from al-Nusra, said rebels who mediated the
talks and an Arab official. They said the talks, sometimes through intermediaries, appear aimed at wooing
some of the Syrian fighters away from al-Qaeda influence into the fold of more moderate factions the U.S.
and its allies seek to strengthen.
The agency has also sent more officers to the Turkish city of Gaziantep and to towns along the border to
monitor areas where extremists are active, according to people familiar with the efforts. They added that
these officers meet frequently with moderate rebel commanders to glean information on al-Nusra's activities
and for battleground updates.
Two floors underground in Raqqa city, behind a green steel door marked "boxing practice," an experiment
with rebel rule offers a glimpse of this Syria beyond government hands.
Islamists have set up what they call a legislative commission to run local affairs. It operates from a bunkerlike
labyrinth of metal doors and narrow hallways beneath Raqqa's sports stadium, safe from airstrikes.
"We're like a little government," said Mohamad al-Da'ayel, a former judge who heads the commission's civil
court.
Residents clutching papers -- rent-dispute forms, appeals, marriage contracts -- crowded the halls on a recent
day as armed men stood guard. In a waiting room, one wall still bore the scribbles and arrows of a soccer
game-plan. Men waited to fill out forms with an Islamic phrase as a letterhead.
Finding themselves ruling a population wary of outsiders, the fundamentalist factions Ahrar and the al-Nusra
Front (in Arabic, Jabhat al-Nusra) invited local professionals and technocrats to help them govern. The
Islamic commission's board has representatives from three other Islamist brigades, all Raqqa natives, as well
as from al-Nusra and Ahrar.

--- Page 138 ---
Page 138 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The challenges facing local members were on display on a recent day. Outside the underground complex,
fighters roamed the streets riding in white pickup trucks with mounted machine guns, their faces hidden
behind black ski masks.
Since March, these fighters have rounded up dozens of residents from homes and street-side cafes, alleging
an array of offenses, residents said. Some of those arrested remain missing.
Al-Nusra and Ahrar deny carrying out unfair kidnappings or detentions. Their fighters say they are protecting
the city from criminals and looters.
Cases that wind up before the Islamic commission often get waived by judges. With no established political
leadership, the judges say it is unlawful to hand down sentences of lashing or execution.
However, earlier this month al-Nusra fighters identifying themselves as part of al Qaeda in Iraq staged a
public execution here of men they said in a video were captured Syrian officers.
On a recent night, al-Nusra and Ahrar fighters raided the headquarters of another brigade, a small one called
Aswad al-Islam, which they accused of looting and kidnapping. They detained the leader and blew up the
building, according to fighters who said they had taken part.
"Women and children wouldn't be safe in Raqqa without Jabhat al-Nusra," said one al-Nusra fighter.
This fighter said he once fought with a group that is part of a Western-backed rebel alliance called the Free
Syrian Army. He moved over to the jihadist al-Nusra late last year and now represents it on the board of
Raqqa's Islamic commission.
Al-Nusra's main rival here is called the al-Farouk Battalions, a part of the Free Syrian Army alliance. Late last
year, they helped take a town on the Turkish border that gave Syrian rebels their foothold in Raqqa province,
as well as a path to an oil-rich province farther east.
Yet five months later, the al-Farouk Battalions found themselves excluded when the extreme Islamist factions
launched the surprise attack on Raqqa city. Now, al-Farouk has no presence in the city's governing structure.
Al-Farouk itself was once considered Islamist-leaning, because most of its fighters were pious Sunnis. But,
hoping for aid and arms from the West, it never built recruitment or strategy around Islamic doctrine.
When the real Islamists emerged, al-Farouk received increased aid from the region and Western countries,
hurting its credibility with some fighters.
"The Farouk were true mujahedeen when they rose from the rubble of Baba Amr," said the al-Nusra fighter,
referring to a pivotal battle in the province of Homs in February 2012. "Now, they're no more than agents
serving the interests of others."
Al-Farouk commanders, in turn, accuse the extremists of abusing Islam to achieve power and control, by
attracting the most hardened fighters and appealing to the masses from a posture of moral and divine
authority.
"Their allegiance to al Qaeda is a state of sickness, a power grab," said Mohamad al-Daher, a 34-year-old
al-Farouk commander for eastern Syria.
The divide burst into open warfare when rebels first made a push into the north of Syria last summer. A
melange of rebel factions took over a border crossing from Turkey called Bab al-Hawa.
Besides the al-Farouk Battalions, there was a group of jihadists, many of them foreigners, known as the
Majlis Shura al-Mujahideen, or Shura Council. Leading this group was a Syrian named Firas al-Absi who had
trained in Afghanistan and was once deported from Saudi Arabia.
Mr. Absi set up a training camp in the Syrian province of Idlib, west of Raqqa. And at the Bab al-Hawa gate,
his group hung the black flag associated with al Qaeda , bearing a white seal of the shahada, the Muslim
profession of faith.
The flag became a major point of contention. Mr. Daher, the al-Farouk commander, said he met repeatedly
with the Shura Council fighters, asking them to replace the flag with one that didn't denote al Qaeda . He said
the flag so rattled local Turkish authorities that they barred ammunition and weapons shipments across the
border for 22 days after it went up.
"We had a battle going on in Homs [province], and we needed every bit of ammo we could get," Mr. Daher
recalled. "We offered to put up a plain black flag or a green flag. But they" -- Mr. Absi and the Shura Council
-- "didn't care."

--- Page 139 ---
Page 139 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The flag fight boiled over at the start of September. Mr. Absi went missing. Three days later, on Sept. 3, his
body was returned.
The al-Farouk Battalions denied involvement in the killing. Some of its fighters now say that they killed Mr.
Absi to quell the threat to their power and the friction in their ties to the Turks. The Shura Council has since
dissolved.
Online jihadist forums started to brand al-Farouk Battalions the "Sahwat" of Syria, in a reference to Iraqi
tribesmen who rose up against al Qaeda in a movement called Iraq's "Sunni Awakening" a few years ago.
Tensions flared again later in September, this time over a much more pivotal border crossing -- a conduit into
the vast agricultural region of eastern Syria.
The region, which includes Raqqa, lies sandwiched between the Tigris and Euphrates rivers and is known as
al-Jazeera, Arabic for island.
Three rebel factions not known as extremist, including the al-Farouk Battalions, pushed into that border
crossing, which is at the town of Tal Abyad in Raqqa Province.
It was around this time that al-Nusra, the extremist group, began to expand its footprint in the region, said
various rebels including the al-Nusra fighter interviewed in Raqqa.
The fighter said he had been part of a unit of the Western-backed Free Syrian Army but switched to al-Nusra
in October when he realized the FSA couldn't supply funds or weapons.
"People don't listen to you when you are weak," he said.
That same month, Mr. Daher, the al-Farouk Battalions eastern commander, said he received his last arms
provisions from the FSA. Though he received 15,000 Kalashnikov bullets, 10,000 rounds for Russian-made
machine guns and 12,000 rocket-propelled grenades, he said, he hasn't received a single bullet since.
Mr. Daher said al-Farouk has had to depend on, among other things, buying light arms from black-market
weapons dealers, running up a $600,000 debt to them. By contrast, the Islamist extremist groups al-Nusra
and Ahrar "became suddenly very strong, together," Mr. Daher said.
On March 4, in Raqqa city, a video taped inside the provincial governor's palace said that a group calling itself
the Front for Islamic Unity and Liberation was declaring the liberation of Raqqa.
The message from this group -- which locals described as mostly a front for al-Nusra and Ahrar -- marked the
start of a brief spate of fighting that would swiftly drive Syrian government forces out of Raqqa city.
Mr. Daher, the al-Farouk commander, was 60 miles away at the time. When he learned of the assault on
Syrian government forces, his fighters joined in the next day and helped to overrun two government
checkpoints.
But his relatively moderate rebels were largely sidelined, as the radicals from al-Nusra and Ahrar had already
deployed their fighters around state institutions, said rebels who led the operation.
Within four days, the last government presence was driven from the city. Ahrar occupied the governor's
palace and took over the central bank branch.
It held six billion Syrian pounds (about $62 million) plus some gold, according to Ahrar representatives. They
said Raqqa's new Islamic commission is working out a system to pay former state employees.
Syrian officials declined to discuss the Raqqa bank branch's holdings.
Watching these events unfold was Omar al-Huwaidi, a 31-year-old Raqqa resident who in the past took part
in antigovernment protests in the city. "The militia takeover of the city . . . was supposed to be liberation, and
then pull out," he said. "Unfortunately, that didn't happen. The fighters, when they took control of the city, they
took everything."
A day after the al Qaeda flag appeared in the city, fighters from the more-moderate al-Farouk faction said
they disabled a 17-pound explosive found under a car outside a home where Mr. Daher, the commander, was
spending the night.
The day after that, the fiercest fight yet between al-Nusra and al-Farouk broke out. Mr. Daher said he headed
to the Turkish border post to check out reports of al-Nusra fighters in pickups roaming around detaining
al-Farouk fighters. As soon he arrived, he said, someone threw a grenade at him and began firing at him. In
the attack, at least two fighters from each side were killed.

--- Page 140 ---
Page 140 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Riddled with shrapnel, Mr. Daher withdrew to Turkey to recover and regroup. In a cafe-turned-safehouse
serving as his new home, on a recent day, he paced the floor in a gray sweatsuit.
"The future is war," he said. "I see a wide-scale war. The Free Syrian Army will fight the Islamists."
---
Suha Ma'ayeh and Siobhan Gorman contributed to this article.

Style & Travel -- Tricks of the Trade: Tiny Touches That Make a Summer Salad Sing
Style & Travel -- Tricks of the Trade: Tiny Touches That Make a Summer Salad Sing
By Cheryl Lu-Lien Tan
1,194 words
30 May 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Sarabeth Levine, Chef, restaurateur]
Summer, with its plethora of fresh ingredients, is the perfect time for entertaining with salads as the star of the
dinner party. "When it's hot out, I don't want to eat hot food," says Sarabeth Levine, owner of six Sarabeth's
restaurants in New York City and Key West, Fla. "I don't want to eat heavy food."
Ms. Levine calls herself "a real salad person" who likes making a meal of it year-round. But she finds herself
particularly inspired to make them the backbone of a dinner party when she is entertaining in warm weather.
"It's the ease of it, the ability to make your guests feel not overly fed. And it's very, very healthy."
When planning an entree salad, Ms. Levine starts with the greens. She typically enjoys using a mix of
lettuces (usually romaine and Boston), sometimes with spinach, a little frisee or steamed asparagus mixed in.
As a rule of thumb, if serving a salad as a main course, she likes having at least "two nice handfuls" of greens
per guest.
"I like having different textures and flavors in the greens," Ms. Levine says. One that she doesn't typically mix
is arugula, because that flavor "is very specific" and works best when standing alone. "When I have arugula in
a salad, I'll maybe add some dried fruit, nothing too crazy."
Once Ms. Levine has her basic greens selected, she'll toss them with fresh herbs -- sometimes snipped from
her own garden -- such as cilantro, Italian parsley and chives. Then she'll round it all out with artichokes
(either in half-inch slices or cubed hearts), cucumbers, roasted peppers and cherry tomatoes. (She prefers
cherry tomatoes to chopped tomatoes, as the juice from a cut-up tomato tends to make the greens soggy.)
Next, Ms. Levine likes to add ingredients that offer oomph. "I love when a salad has some form of a bean or
grain. It's more complete," she says. "The last thing you want your guests to feel is that you're putting them
on a diet. This is not about being on a diet. It's about offering them something that tastes good."
Ms. Levine often adds couscous, wheat berry or barley. It's important to "really wash the starchiness out of
the barley" after cooking by rinsing it in cold water so the grains don't stick together.
Ms. Levine believes in scouring local markets for the freshest seasonal fruit to add to salads. She favors
apples, cherries, mangoes, peaches or pears. "You want fleshy fruit," she says. "It gives the salad a little
sweetness, tanginess and a little chew."
A recent discovery she made was tiny gold-nugget mandarin oranges, which she likes because they're
seedless and sweet. "I took the orange segments and sliced them horizontally and placed them on top of the
salad. It was so delicious and juicy," she says.
Nuts are a good way of jazzing up a salad. Ms. Levine favors toasted hazelnuts, mild candied walnuts or
smoked almonds, which add flavor and a nice crunch to greens. "Peanuts are too strong, and cashews are
pretty heavy in a salad," she notes.
Croutons are an excellent topper. Ms. Levine likes cutting a mild, light bread such as country white or pain de
mie -- nothing too hard, she says -- into half-inch cubes, tossing them in melted butter mixed with thyme and
garlic, and baking them lightly.
For an entree salad, Ms. Levine always adds a protein. Poached salmon and roast chicken (sometimes a
purchased rotisserie chicken, chopped up) work well.
Often she will turn to fried calamari or scallops. "Lobster is always well received," she notes.

--- Page 142 ---
Page 142 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Then comes the important part -- the dressing. "The dressing has to hold up to the ingredients of the salad,"
Ms. Levine says. "You don't want your salad to be weighted down by the dressing, but you want to be able to
taste it."
Ms. Levine favors dressings that are a little lighter in the summer, although she notes that "hearty leaves with
strong cellular structure hold up very nicely to thicker dressing."
For a basic arugula salad, she tends to keep the dressing simple, mixing lemon juice with olive oil blended
with some canola oil. Canola is lighter than olive oil and lightens up a dressing. The citrus note will help cut
the strong flavor of the arugula, she says.
Sometimes she blends mustard, vinegar, olive oil and canola or corn oil in a food processor with herbs such
as basil, parsley or oregano. With salmon, she likes to add a touch of dill to the dressing; with chicken, she
typically adds some tarragon.
A recent go-to for Ms. Levine is a buttermilk dressing featuring Italian parsley, celery seed and tarragon or
dill. Toward the end, she'll whisk in a French sheep feta -- a cheese she says she has "fallen madly in love
with."
"It doesn't taste like feta in the way that you think of feta," she says. "It has a nice tanginess, and it's not salty.
It's not dry." Although she'll sometimes whisk in a blue cheese instead, she's careful to use a "very mild" blue.
Many blue cheeses can "overpower the dressing," she says.
Once all the ingredients have been prepped, Ms. Levine is careful to "keep the greens in the refrigerator until
the very last minute, so when I make the salad, everything is very cold."
Then, she'll set out the greens in a large bowl, artfully arranging the cherry tomatoes in a mound in the center
-- "like an eyeball," she says, noting that they add color to the presentation.
Because she likes to avoid having guests dig through the salad for ingredients they like, she'll place heftier
items, like artichokes, peppers and olives, in small mounds circling the tomatoes, so they can be seen
immediately. "Olives always get lost in the salad and wind up at the bottom of the bowl," she notes.
She'll place the protein and nuts in a dish on the side, so guests can add them as they wish.
Finally, it's essential to leave the dressing on the side. She usually serves it in a large jar. "If you dress the
salad, after 20 minutes, it's soggy," she says.
But more important, "dressing on a salad is a very personal thing," she adds. "No two people like the same
amount of dressing."
---
How You Know Her
-- Started out making orange-apricot marmalade in her home kitchen. She sold it to New York City food
shops, then in 1981 opened a small bakery selling preserves and baked goods with her husband and
business partner, Bill Levine.
-- Opened Sarabeth's (East) restaurant in New York City in 1983. Now has six restaurants in New York and
Key West, Fla.
-- Preserves and spreads are sold in U.S. department stores and specialty shops, as well as in the
Caribbean, Canada, Japan and South Korea.

Stocks Change Their Fuel
Tuesday's Markets
Stocks Change Their Fuel
By Jonathan Cheng
805 words
29 May 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A knotty market mystery unraveled Tuesday, as strong reports on U.S. home prices and consumer
confidence sent investors rushing to buy economically sensitive stocks and dump Treasurys.
The broad rally in stocks pushed the Dow Jones Industrial Average up 106.29 points, or 0.69%, to a new high
of 15409.39, while giving the blue-chip measure its 20th consecutive Tuesday of gains.
The move helped answer a persistent market head-scratcher of 2013: how long could unglamorous
"defensive" stocks such as utilities and telecommunications companies power the unexpectedly robust
stock-market rally?
So far this year, the Dow has moved 18% higher without even a three-day string of losses, in large part due
to investors' predilection for defensive stocks, which are less sensitive to the rocky economic recovery and
deliver steady, attractive yields.
Among the 15 worst-performing stocks Tuesday on the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, all but three were
utilities and telecommunications companies, or real-estate investment trusts. Those 12 stocks, each of which
lost 1.9% or more amid the general stock-market advance, average a dividend yield of nearly 5%, more than
double the S&P 500's dividend yield of 2.1%.
The high-yielding stocks slumped as investors, acting increasingly optimistic about the U.S. economic
recovery, dumped U.S. Treasurys. The drop in Treasury prices pushed the yield on the benchmark 10-year
note -- which moves inversely to price -- up to 2.132% in 3 p.m. New York trading, the highest level since
April 5, 2012.
The sharp rise in Treasury yields -- the steepest intraday gain in more than a year, according to Tradeweb
data -- is potentially detrimental to high-dividend stocks because it provides investors with an alternative as
they search for stable streams of income.
"When Treasury yields start to rise, these stocks lose their luster," said Robert Pavlik , chief market strategist
at Banyan Partners .
After a hot start to the year by utilities and telecoms stocks, investors in recent weeks began to see these
high-dividend payers as expensive relative to the broader market and, in particular, to cyclical sectors such as
technology and industrial companies.
In mid-April, Phil Orlando , who helps manage about $380 billion as chief market strategist at Federated
Investors, turned bearish on these sectors and increased his firm's exposure to cyclical stocks.
"The rally in the defensives is done," Mr. Orlando said on Tuesday.
After a three-day weekend, U.S. investors came into Tuesday's session with a strong wind at their backs,
following stock rallies in Europe and Japan. They got a further boost after the release of two strong economic
reports, showing U.S. home prices jumping by their biggest margin in seven years and consumer confidence
rising to a five-year high.
"I see a lot of tailwinds for the rally to continue," said Jerry Harris , chief investment officer of the
asset-management arm of Birmingham, Ala.-based Sterne Agee, which manages about $17 billion in assets.
"Gas prices are way down from where they were a few months ago, and house prices are way up."
Kristina Hooper, head of portfolio strategies at Allianz Global Investors in New York, which manages about
$400 billion, is more cautious.

--- Page 144 ---
Page 144 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"This has been such a defensive-driven market, and today may well be the beginning of a rotation to more
cyclical stocks, which would bolster this idea that this rally has legs to run," Ms. Hooper said. At the same
time, she added, investors remain concerned about the Federal Reserve and its commitment to stimulating
the economy with bond purchases.
Last week, the Dow suffered its first weekly stumble in four weeks amid concerns about the Fed withdrawing
its stimulus, and whether similar efforts by the Bank of Japan would be effective.
Leading Tuesday's gains were financial, energy and consumer-discretionary stocks. Among Dow
components, Microsoft , UnitedHealth and Walt Disney led the benchmark higher, rising 2.2%, 2.1% and
1.8% respectively. AT&T and Verizon Communications , which pay the highest dividends among the 30 Dow
industrials, shed 1.5% and 1.1% each.
Among S&P 500 components, utilities operators Exelon dropped 7.5% and FirstEnergy tumbled 6.5% to lead
the decliners.
Meanwhile, U.S.-listed shares of Valeant Pharmaceuticals surged 8.7% after the company said Monday that
it agreed to buy privately held Bausch & Lomb for $8.7 billion.
Tiffany rose 4% after the luxury jeweler reported quarterly earnings and revenue that exceeded analyst
forecasts, amid broad global sales growth, and kept its full-year outlook unchanged.
Crude-oil futures gained 0.9% to $95.01 a barrel, while gold futures slipped 0.6% to $1,379.10 an ounce.
The dollar surged against the euro and the yen.

Central Banks Stock Up On Gold as Prices Drop
Central Banks Stock Up On Gold as Prices Drop
By Clementine Wallop and Rhiannon Hoyle
376 words
28 May 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C7
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Russia, Kazakhstan and Azerbaijan boosted their gold holdings in April, a month that saw prices plunge to
two-year lows in a pullback that raised questions over the metal's haven status but also offered an opportunity
to buy into the market at lower costs.
The International Monetary Fund issued its monthly gold-buying report on Monday, which reflects the
activities of almost all central banks and is closely watched by gold investors. The report showed that the
three former Soviet republics increased their holdings by a cumulative 75% in April from March. Official
purchasing has provided important support for gold prices over the past few years. According to the World
Gold Council , central-bank buying represented 11% of all gold demand in the first quarter of this year.
Altogether, the IMF data show that central-bank holdings rose 972,000 ounces last month.
Gold prices shed as much as 17% in April and ended the month 7.5% lower. It is trading at about $1,390 a
troy ounce, down 12% since the start of the year.
Despite overall net buying, central-bank activity was also an important factor in gold's plunge, especially the
news on April 10 that Cyprus was considering selling 10 tons of gold from its reserves to raise cash. Cyprus's
move stoked fears that other debt-laden European nations -- including Italy, which has the world's
fourth-largest gold reserves -- could follow suit.
"I think the news [of central-bank buying] will help to steady the gold price decline," Beijing Antaike gold
analyst Yvonne Wang said. "It shows that some people still have faith in gold."
Fund managers' short positions in gold -- bets the price would fall -- were at a record 79,416 contracts in the
week ended May 21, Commodity Futures Trading Commission data showed Friday.
Speculative investors added 1,454 long positions -- bets that prices would rise -- against 4,985 short
positions, the CFTC said.
Given the large number of short positions and recent heavy liquidation from gold exchange-traded funds,
gold has limited upside for now, said Antaike's Ms. Wang. "Gold is still lacking drivers that will push it
higher."

The Intelligent Investor: The Japan Syndrome: Rising Rates and Risky Exposures
The Intelligent Investor: The Japan Syndrome: Rising Rates and Risky Exposures
By Jason Zweig
851 words
25 May 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
That was close.
The U.S. stock market escaped the selloff in the Japanese market -- at least for now. When the Nikkei Stock
Average dropped 7.3% this Thursday, the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell by as much as 1.1% during
the day, only to finish down a hair.
But the plunge in Japan, triggered partly by fears that interest rates might rise, is a wake-up call for U.S.
investors. It is worth peering inside your portfolio to evaluate which assets could be most vulnerable once the
Federal Reserve finally stops keeping interest rates unnaturally low.
Many companies whose stocks have recently been wildly popular for their income and safety -- and that have
gorged on cheap debt -- could suddenly turn into pariahs.
"Highly leveraged companies [those with high levels of debt] have done extremely well in the past three
years," says Harindra de Silva, a portfolio manager at Analytic Investors, an investment firm in Los Angeles
that manages $8 billion. "But they have the potential to really hurt you going forward."
A rotation already might be under way; suddenly, the "safety trade" is faltering. Real-estate investment trusts,
utility stocks, high-dividend companies and "low-volatility" stocks (which move up and down less sharply than
the market as a whole) all have been performing spectacularly well -- until this month, when they started to
fade.
Many such companies, which investors have been buying for their perceived safety and income, have
borrowed heavily.
The S&P 500, up 12.7% in the first four months, has added another 3.5% in May. Stocks with high dividends,
as measured by the SPDR S&P Dividend exchange-traded fund, crushed the market with a 16.4% return
through April; in May, however, they fell behind, adding less than 3%. Low-volatility stocks, up 15% and more
until this month, stayed flat in May. REITs, with a 15.4% gain through April, have lost 1.2% so far this month.
And utilities, which returned 19.9% in the first four months, have fallen 5.6% in May.
"The conservative stocks had done so well and gotten so expensive," Mr. de Silva says, "that it's almost like
people have started saying, 'Well, if everything's going to go up, I might as well buy the cheaper, riskier
stocks now.'"
Consider that at the end of April, utilities were trading at 22.9 times their earnings over the past year and
low-volatility stocks at 24.9 times, versus 21.2 times for the S&P 500. High-dividend payers were trading at
17.2 times their projected earnings over the next year, with the S&P at 15.1 times next year's estimates.
History shows that whatever is stretched tends to tear when interest rates rise.
On Feb. 4, 1994, the Fed jacked up rates in the first of what would turn out to be five increases that year.
Then, as now, the backdrop was a period in which "money the Fed has been so generously creating has
been flowing into securities . . . and may have been creating a securities market bubble," as The Wall Street
Journal wrote the day after the first rise in rates.
That day, the Dow Jones Industrial Average tanked 2.4% -- "partly," wrote the Journal, "because of
uncertainty about how far the Fed will go."
The biggest fear of investors then was that the Fed would tank the markets, which still were recovering from
the recession of 1990-91. That didn't quite happen; the U.S. stock market overall earned 1.3% for the full year
in 1994.

--- Page 147 ---
Page 147 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But the damage was widespread, hitting supposedly safe and risky assets alike. For the full year, utility stocks
lost 15.3%; municipal bonds, 5.2%; emerging markets, 8.7%; intermediate-term Treasurys, 5.7%; the U.S.
bond market as a whole, 2.9%; gold, 2.2%. REITs eked out a gain of 0.8%; without their generous dividend
yield of roughly 7%, they would have lost 6.4%. (The average yield on REITs today is a bit over 3%.)
There was no index of low-volatility stocks back in 1994. Mr. de Silva's research shows, however, that in
periods of rising interest rates, these stocks have tended to underperform the market as a whole by an
average of about 0.8 percentage point annually.
This past week's tumble in Japan confirms the lessons of 1994. Just the thought of what will happen when a
central bank tightens the faucet of easy money can spook many investors.
REITs, dividend-paying companies, utilities and low-volatility stocks all have a place in a portfolio. But they
aren't worth paying a premium price for -- and, as safe as they have seemed until recently, they won't offer
blanket protection once the fear of rising rates becomes reality.
The many investors who have been overloading their portfolios with this kind of safety may soon be sorry.
